class="infobox-label" scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Electronegativity" title="Electronegativity">Electronegativity</a></th><td class="infobox-data">Pauling scale: 1.90 </td></tr><tr><th </li></ul></div></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Atomic_radius" title="Atomic radius">Atomic radius</a></th><td class="infobox-data">empirical: 128 <a .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Infobox_copper" title="Template:Infobox copper"><span title="View this template">view</span></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Infobox_copper" title="Template talk:Infobox copper"><span title="Discuss this elements">references</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Copper</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">chemical element</a> with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Symbol_(chemistry)" title="Symbol (chemistry)">symbol</a> <b>Cu</b> (from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <i lang="la">cuprum</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Atomic_number" title="Atomic number">atomic number</a> 29. It is a soft, malleable, and <a href="/wiki/Ductility" title="Ductility">ductile</a> metal with very high <a href="/wiki/Thermal_conductivity" title="Thermal conductivity">thermal</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_conductivity" title="Electrical conductivity">electrical conductivity</a>. A freshly exposed surface of pure copper has a <a href="/wiki/Copper_(color)" title="Copper (color)">pinkish-orange color</a>. Copper is used as a conductor of heat and electricity, as a <a href="/wiki/Building_material#Metal" title="Building material">building material</a>, and as a constituent of various metal <a href="/wiki/Alloy" title="Alloy">alloys</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Sterling_silver" title="Sterling silver">sterling silver</a> used in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jewelry" title="Jewelry">jewelry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cupronickel" title="Cupronickel">cupronickel</a> used to make marine hardware and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coins" title="Coins">coins</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Constantan" title="Constantan">constantan</a> used in <a href="/wiki/Strain_gauge" title="Strain gauge">strain gauges</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thermocouples" title="Thermocouples">thermocouples</a> for temperature measurement.
</p><p>Copper is one of the few metals that can occur in nature in a directly usable metallic form (<a href="/wiki/Native_metal" title="Native metal">native metals</a>). This led to very early human use in several regions, from circa 8000 BC. Thousands of years later, it was the first metal to be <a href="/wiki/Smelting" title="Smelting">smelted</a> from sulfide ores, circa 5000 BC; the first metal to be cast into a shape in a mold, c. 4000 BC; and the first metal to be purposely alloyed with another metal, <a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">tin</a>, to create <a href="/wiki/Bronze" title="Bronze">bronze</a>, c. 3500 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EncBrit_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-EncBrit-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Roman era</a>, copper was mined principally on <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a>, the origin of the name of the metal, from <i>aes cyprium</i> (metal of Cyprus), later corrupted to <i>cuprum</i> (Latin). <i>Coper</i> (<a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a>) and <i>copper</i> were derived from this, the later spelling first used around 1530.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>Commonly encountered compounds are copper(II) salts, which often impart blue or green colors to such minerals as <a href="/wiki/Azurite" title="Azurite">azurite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malachite" title="Malachite">malachite</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Turquoise" title="Turquoise">turquoise</a>, and have been used widely and historically as pigments.
</p><p>Copper used in buildings, usually for roofing, oxidizes to form a green <a href="/wiki/Verdigris" title="Verdigris">verdigris</a> (or <a href="/wiki/Patina" title="Patina">patina</a>). Copper is sometimes used in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decorative_art" title="Decorative art">decorative art</a>, both in its elemental metal form and in compounds as pigments. Copper compounds are used as <a href="/wiki/Bacteriostatic_agent" title="Bacteriostatic agent">bacteriostatic agents</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fungicide" title="Fungicide">fungicides</a>, and wood preservatives.
</p><p>Copper is essential to all living organisms as a trace <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dietary_mineral" title="Dietary mineral">dietary mineral</a> because it is a key constituent of the respiratory enzyme complex <a href="/wiki/Cytochrome_c_oxidase" title="Cytochrome c oxidase">cytochrome c oxidase</a>. In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molluscs" title="Molluscs">molluscs</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crustacea" title="Crustacea">crustaceans</a>, copper is a constituent of the blood pigment <a href="/wiki/Hemocyanin" title="Hemocyanin">hemocyanin</a>, replaced by the iron-complexed <a href="/wiki/Hemoglobin" title="Hemoglobin">hemoglobin</a> in fish and other <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrates</a>. In humans, copper is found mainly in the liver, muscle, and bone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> The adult body contains between 1.4 and 2.1 mg of copper per kilogram of body weight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-34"><a href="#Biological_role"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Biological role</span></a>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical">Physical</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cu-Scheibe.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3584" data-file-width="3616" decoding="async" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Cu-Scheibe.JPG/150px-Cu-Scheibe.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Cu-Scheibe.JPG/225px-Cu-Scheibe.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Cu-Scheibe.JPG/300px-Cu-Scheibe.JPG 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cu-Scheibe.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A copper disc (99.95% pure) made by <a href="/wiki/Continuous_casting" title="Continuous casting">continuous casting</a>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Industrial_etching" title="Industrial etching">etched</a> to reveal <a href="/wiki/Crystallite" title="Crystallite">crystallites</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Molten_copper_in_bright_sunlight.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="196" data-file-width="306" decoding="async" height="96" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Molten_copper_in_bright_sunlight.gif/150px-Molten_copper_in_bright_sunlight.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Molten_copper_in_bright_sunlight.gif/225px-Molten_copper_in_bright_sunlight.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Molten_copper_in_bright_sunlight.gif/300px-Molten_copper_in_bright_sunlight.gif 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Molten_copper_in_bright_sunlight.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Copper just above its melting point keeps its pink luster color when enough light outshines the orange <a href="/wiki/Incandescence" title="Incandescence">incandescence</a> color</div></div></div>
<p>Copper, <a href="/wiki/Silver" title="Silver">silver</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">gold</a> are in <a href="/wiki/Group_11_element" title="Group 11 element">group 11</a> of the periodic table; these three metals have one s-orbital electron on top of a filled d-<a href="/wiki/Electron_shell" title="Electron shell">electron shell</a> and are characterized by high <a href="/wiki/Ductility" title="Ductility">ductility</a>, and electrical and thermal conductivity. The filled d-shells in these elements contribute little to interatomic interactions, which are dominated by the s-electrons through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metallic_bond" title="Metallic bond">metallic bonds</a>. Unlike metals with incomplete d-shells, metallic bonds in copper are lacking a <a href="/wiki/Covalent_bond" title="Covalent bond">covalent</a> character and are relatively weak. This observation explains the low <a href="/wiki/Hardness" title="Hardness">hardness</a> and high ductility of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monocrystalline" title="Monocrystalline">single crystals</a> of copper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-b1_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-b1-9">[9]</a></sup> At the macroscopic scale, introduction of extended defects to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crystal_lattice" title="Crystal lattice">crystal lattice</a>, such as grain boundaries, hinders flow of the material under applied stress, thereby increasing its hardness. For this reason, copper is usually supplied in a fine-grained <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polycrystalline" title="Polycrystalline">polycrystalline</a> form, which has greater strength than monocrystalline forms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>The softness of copper partly explains its high electrical conductivity (59.6×10<sup>6</sup> <a href="/wiki/Siemens_(unit)" title="Siemens (unit)">S</a>/m) and high thermal conductivity, second highest (second only to silver) among pure metals at room temperature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRC_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-11">[11]</a></sup> This is because the resistivity to electron transport in metals at room temperature originates primarily from scattering of electrons on thermal vibrations of the lattice, which are relatively weak in a soft metal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-b1_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-b1-9">[9]</a></sup> The maximum permissible current density of copper in open air is approximately 3.1×10<sup>6</sup> A/m<sup>2</sup> of cross-sectional area, above which it begins to heat excessively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>Copper is one of a few metallic elements with a natural color other than gray or silver.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Pure copper is orange-red and acquires a reddish <a href="/wiki/Tarnish" title="Tarnish">tarnish</a> when exposed to air. The characteristic color of copper results from the electronic transitions between the filled 3d and half-empty 4s atomic shells – the energy difference between these shells corresponds to orange light.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>As with other metals, if copper is put in contact with another metal, <a href="/wiki/Galvanic_corrosion" title="Galvanic corrosion">galvanic corrosion</a> will occur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical">Chemical</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Copper_wire_comparison.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="297" data-file-width="285" decoding="async" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Copper_wire_comparison.JPG/150px-Copper_wire_comparison.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Copper_wire_comparison.JPG/225px-Copper_wire_comparison.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b6/Copper_wire_comparison.JPG 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Copper_wire_comparison.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Unoxidized copper wire (left) and oxidized copper wire (right)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Royal_Observatory_Edinburgh_East_Tower_2010_cropped.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="570" data-file-width="801" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Royal_Observatory_Edinburgh_East_Tower_2010_cropped.jpg/220px-Royal_Observatory_Edinburgh_East_Tower_2010_cropped.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Royal_Observatory_Edinburgh_East_Tower_2010_cropped.jpg/330px-Royal_Observatory_Edinburgh_East_Tower_2010_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Royal_Observatory_Edinburgh_East_Tower_2010_cropped.jpg/440px-Royal_Observatory_Edinburgh_East_Tower_2010_cropped.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Royal_Observatory_Edinburgh_East_Tower_2010_cropped.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The East Tower of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Observatory,_Edinburgh" title="Royal Observatory, Edinburgh">Royal Observatory, Edinburgh</a>, showing the contrast between the refurbished copper installed in 2010 and the green color of the original 1894 copper.</div></div></div>
<p>Copper does not react with water, but it does slowly react with atmospheric oxygen to form a layer of brown-black copper oxide which, unlike the <a href="/wiki/Rust" title="Rust">rust</a> that forms on iron in moist air, protects the underlying metal from further corrosion (<a href="/wiki/Passivation_(chemistry)" title="Passivation (chemistry)">passivation</a>). A green layer of <a href="/wiki/Verdigris" title="Verdigris">verdigris</a> (copper carbonate) can often be seen on old copper structures, such as the roofing of many older buildings<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-15">[15]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Statue_of_Liberty" title="Statue of Liberty">Statue of Liberty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Copper <a href="/wiki/Tarnish" title="Tarnish">tarnishes</a> when exposed to some <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> compounds, with which it reacts to form various <a href="/wiki/Copper_sulfide" title="Copper sulfide">copper sulfides</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Isotopes">Isotopes</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Isotopes_of_copper" title="Isotopes of copper">Isotopes of copper</a></div>
<p>There are 29 <a href="/wiki/Isotope" title="Isotope">isotopes</a> of copper. <sup>63</sup>Cu and <sup>65</sup>Cu are stable, with <sup>63</sup>Cu comprising approximately 69% of naturally occurring copper; both have a <a href="/wiki/Spin_(physics)" title="Spin (physics)">spin</a> of <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1020198016">.mw-parser-output .frac{white-space:nowrap}.mw-parser-output .frac .num,.mw-parser-output .frac .den{font-size:80%;line-height:0;vertical-align:super}.mw-parser-output .frac .den{vertical-align:sub}.mw-parser-output .sr-only{border:0;clip:rect(0,0,0,0);height:1px;margin:-1px;overflow:hidden;padding:0;position:absolute;width:1px}</style><span class="frac" role="math"><span class="num">3</span>⁄<span class="den">2</span></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nubase_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-nubase-18">[18]</a></sup> The other isotopes are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioactivity" title="Radioactivity">radioactive</a>, with the most stable being <sup>67</sup>Cu with a <a href="/wiki/Half-life" title="Half-life">half-life</a> of 61.83 hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nubase_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-nubase-18">[18]</a></sup> Seven <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_isomer" title="Nuclear isomer">metastable isotopes</a> have been characterized; <sup>68m</sup>Cu is the longest-lived with a half-life of 3.8 minutes. Isotopes with a <a href="/wiki/Mass_number" title="Mass number">mass number</a> above 64 decay by <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">β<sup>−</sup></a>, whereas those with a mass number below 64 decay by <a href="/wiki/Positron_emission" title="Positron emission">β<sup>+</sup></a>. <a href="/wiki/Copper-64" title="Copper-64"><sup>64</sup>Cu</a>, which has a half-life of 12.7 hours, decays both ways.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p><sup>62</sup>Cu and <sup>64</sup>Cu have significant applications. <sup>62</sup>Cu is used in <sup>62</sup>Cu-PTSM as a <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_tracer" title="Radioactive tracer">radioactive tracer</a> for <a href="/wiki/Positron_emission_tomography" title="Positron emission tomography">positron emission tomography</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence">Occurrence</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_copper_ores" title="List of copper ores">Copper ores</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Native_Copper_from_the_Keweenaw_Peninsula_Michigan.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" decoding="async" height="113" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d8/Native_Copper_from_the_Keweenaw_Peninsula_Michigan.jpg/150px-Native_Copper_from_the_Keweenaw_Peninsula_Michigan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d8/Native_Copper_from_the_Keweenaw_Peninsula_Michigan.jpg/225px-Native_Copper_from_the_Keweenaw_Peninsula_Michigan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d8/Native_Copper_from_the_Keweenaw_Peninsula_Michigan.jpg/300px-Native_Copper_from_the_Keweenaw_Peninsula_Michigan.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Native_Copper_from_the_Keweenaw_Peninsula_Michigan.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Native copper from the Keweenaw Peninsula, Michigan, about 2.5 inches (6.4 cm) long</div></div></div>
<p>Copper is produced in massive stars<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> and is present in the Earth's crust in a proportion of about 50 parts per million (ppm).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emsley_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-emsley-22">[22]</a></sup> In nature, copper occurs in a variety of minerals, including <a href="/wiki/Native_copper" title="Native copper">native copper</a>, copper sulfides such as <a href="/wiki/Chalcopyrite" title="Chalcopyrite">chalcopyrite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bornite" title="Bornite">bornite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Digenite" title="Digenite">digenite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Covellite" title="Covellite">covellite</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chalcocite" title="Chalcocite">chalcocite</a>, copper <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sulfosalt_minerals" title="Sulfosalt minerals">sulfosalts</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Tetrahedrite" title="Tetrahedrite">tetrahedite-tennantite</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Enargite" title="Enargite">enargite</a>, copper carbonates such as <a href="/wiki/Azurite" title="Azurite">azurite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malachite" title="Malachite">malachite</a>, and as copper(I) or copper(II) oxides such as <a href="/wiki/Cuprite" title="Cuprite">cuprite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tenorite" title="Tenorite">tenorite</a>, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRC_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-11">[11]</a></sup> The largest mass of elemental copper discovered weighed 420 tonnes and was found in 1857 on the <a href="/wiki/Keweenaw_Peninsula" title="Keweenaw Peninsula">Keweenaw Peninsula</a> in Michigan, US.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emsley_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-emsley-22">[22]</a></sup> Native copper is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polycrystal" title="Polycrystal">polycrystal</a>, with the largest single crystal ever described measuring 4.4×3.2×3.2 cm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Copper is the 25th most abundant element in <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_crust" title="Earth's crust">Earth's crust</a>, representing 50 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Part_per_million" title="Part per million">ppm</a> compared with 75 ppm for <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a>, and 14 ppm for <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>Typical background concentrations of copper do not exceed 1 ng/m<sup>3</sup> in the atmosphere; 150 mg/kg in soil; 30 mg/kg in vegetation; 2 μg/L in freshwater and 0.5 μg/L in seawater.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chuquicamata-002.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1369" data-file-width="2126" decoding="async" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Chuquicamata-002.jpg/220px-Chuquicamata-002.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Chuquicamata-002.jpg/330px-Chuquicamata-002.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Chuquicamata-002.jpg/440px-Chuquicamata-002.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chuquicamata-002.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Chuquicamata" title="Chuquicamata">Chuquicamata</a>, in Chile, is one of the world's largest <a href="/wiki/Open-pit_mining" title="Open-pit mining">open pit</a> copper <a href="/wiki/Mining" title="Mining">mines</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Copper_-_world_production_trend.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="530" data-file-width="750" decoding="async" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Copper_-_world_production_trend.svg/220px-Copper_-_world_production_trend.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Copper_-_world_production_trend.svg/330px-Copper_-_world_production_trend.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Copper_-_world_production_trend.svg/440px-Copper_-_world_production_trend.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Copper_-_world_production_trend.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>World production trend</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_copper_production" title="List of countries by copper production">List of countries by copper production</a></div>
<p>Most copper is mined or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Copper_extraction_techniques" title="Copper extraction techniques">extracted</a> as copper sulfides from large <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Open_pit_mine" title="Open pit mine">open pit mines</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Porphyry_copper" title="Porphyry copper">porphyry copper</a> deposits that contain 0.4 to 1.0% copper. Sites include <a href="/wiki/Chuquicamata" title="Chuquicamata">Chuquicamata</a>, in Chile, <a href="/wiki/Bingham_Canyon_Mine" title="Bingham Canyon Mine">Bingham Canyon Mine</a>, in Utah, United States, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/El_Chino_Mine" title="El Chino Mine">El Chino Mine</a>, in New Mexico, United States. According to the <a href="/wiki/British_Geological_Survey" title="British Geological Survey">British Geological Survey</a>, in 2005, Chile was the top producer of copper with at least one-third of the world share followed by the United States, Indonesia and Peru.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CRC_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-11">[11]</a></sup> Copper can also be recovered through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/In-situ_leach" title="In-situ leach">in-situ leach</a> process. Several sites in the state of Arizona are considered prime candidates for this method.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> The amount of copper in use is increasing and the quantity available is barely sufficient to allow all countries to reach developed world levels of usage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> An alternative source of copper for <a href="/wiki/Deep_sea_mining" title="Deep sea mining">collection</a> currently being researched are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polymetallic_nodules" title="Polymetallic nodules">polymetallic nodules</a>, which are located at the depths of the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific Ocean</a> approximately 3000–6500 meters below sea level. These nodules contain other valuable metals such as <a href="/wiki/Cobalt" title="Cobalt">cobalt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">nickel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reserves_and_prices">Reserves and prices</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Peak_copper#Reserves" title="Peak copper">Peak copper § Reserves</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:327px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Price_of_Copper.webp"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1482" data-file-width="5400" decoding="async" height="89" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Price_of_Copper.webp/325px-Price_of_Copper.webp.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Price_of_Copper.webp/488px-Price_of_Copper.webp.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Price_of_Copper.webp/650px-Price_of_Copper.webp.png 2x" width="325"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Price_of_Copper.webp" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Price of Copper 1959-2022</div></div></div>
<p>Copper has been in use at least 10,000 years, but more than 95% of all copper ever mined and <a href="/wiki/Smelting" title="Smelting">smelted</a> has been extracted since 1900.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leonard2006_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Leonard2006-29">[29]</a></sup> As with many natural resources, the total amount of copper on Earth is vast, with around 10<sup>14</sup> tons in the top kilometer of Earth's crust, which is about 5 million years' worth at the current rate of extraction. However, only a tiny fraction of these reserves is economically viable with present-day prices and technologies. Estimates of copper reserves available for mining vary from 25 to 60 years, depending on core assumptions such as the growth rate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> Recycling is a major source of copper in the modern world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leonard2006_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Leonard2006-29">[29]</a></sup> Because of these and other factors, the future of copper production and supply is the subject of much debate, including the concept of <a href="/wiki/Peak_copper" title="Peak copper">peak copper</a>, analogous to <a href="/wiki/Peak_oil" title="Peak oil">peak oil</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The price of copper has historically been unstable,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> and its price increased from the 60-year low of US$0.60/lb (US$1.32/kg) in June 1999 to $3.75 per pound ($8.27/kg) in May 2006. It dropped to $2.40/lb ($5.29/kg) in February 2007, then rebounded to $3.50/lb ($7.71/kg) in April 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This page is useless for verification as it is updated hourly. Archives exist, but they do not go back far enough and in any case the graphs are not shown with the precision quoted (April 2014)">better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In February 2009, weakening global demand and a steep fall in commodity prices since the previous year's highs left copper prices at $1.51/lb ($3.32/kg).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Between September 2010 and February 2011, the price of copper rose from £5,000 a metric ton to £6,250 a metric ton.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Methods">Methods</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Copper_extraction_techniques" title="Copper extraction techniques">Copper extraction techniques</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Copper_Flash_Smelting_Process_(EN).svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1470" data-file-width="980" decoding="async" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Copper_Flash_Smelting_Process_%28EN%29.svg/220px-Copper_Flash_Smelting_Process_%28EN%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Copper_Flash_Smelting_Process_%28EN%29.svg/330px-Copper_Flash_Smelting_Process_%28EN%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Copper_Flash_Smelting_Process_%28EN%29.svg/440px-Copper_Flash_Smelting_Process_%28EN%29.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Copper_Flash_Smelting_Process_(EN).svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Scheme of flash smelting process</div></div></div>
<p>The concentration of copper in ores averages only 0.6%, and most commercial ores are sulfides, especially chalcopyrite (CuFeS<sub>2</sub>), bornite (Cu<sub>5</sub>FeS<sub>4</sub>) and, to a lesser extent, covellite (CuS) and chalcocite (Cu<sub>2</sub>S).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-G&amp;E_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-G&amp;E-35">[35]</a></sup> Conversely, the average concentration of copper in polymetallic nodules is estimated at 1.3%. The methods of extracting copper as well as other metals found in these nodules include sulphuric leaching, <a href="/wiki/Smelting" title="Smelting">smelting</a> and an application of the Cuprion process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> For minerals found in land ores, they are concentrated from <a href="/wiki/Comminution" title="Comminution">crushed</a> ores to the level of 10–15% copper by <a href="/wiki/Froth_flotation" title="Froth flotation">froth flotation</a> or <a href="/wiki/Bioleaching" title="Bioleaching">bioleaching</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Heating this material with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Silica" title="Silica">silica</a> in <a href="/wiki/Flash_smelting" title="Flash smelting">flash smelting</a> removes much of the iron as <a href="/wiki/Slag" title="Slag">slag</a>. The process exploits the greater ease of converting iron sulfides into oxides, which in turn react with the silica to form the <a href="/wiki/Silicate" title="Silicate">silicate</a> slag that floats on top of the heated mass. The resulting <i>copper matte,</i> consisting of Cu<sub>2</sub>S, is <a href="/wiki/Roasting_(metallurgy)" title="Roasting (metallurgy)">roasted</a> to convert the sulfides into oxides:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-G&amp;E_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-G&amp;E-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>2 Cu<sub>2</sub>S + 3 O<sub>2</sub> → 2 Cu<sub>2</sub>O + 2 SO<sub>2</sub></dd></dl>
<p>The cuprous oxide reacts with cuprous sulfide to converted to <i>blister</i> copper upon heating:
</p>
<dl><dd>2 Cu<sub>2</sub>O + Cu<sub>2</sub>S → 6 Cu + 2 SO<sub>2</sub></dd></dl>
<p>The Sudbury <a href="/wiki/Matte_(metallurgy)" title="Matte (metallurgy)">matte</a> process converted only half the sulfide to oxide and then used this oxide to remove the rest of the sulfur as oxide. It was then electrolytically refined and the anode mud exploited for the <a href="/wiki/Platinum" title="Platinum">platinum</a> and gold it contained. This step exploits the relatively easy reduction of copper oxides to copper metal. <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">Natural gas</a> is blown across the blister to remove most of the remaining oxygen and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrorefining" title="Electrorefining">electrorefining</a> is performed on the resulting material to produce pure copper:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>Cu<sup>2+</sup> + 2 e<sup>−</sup> → Cu</dd></dl>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1062633282">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .content .thumb>div:not(.thumbinner){display:flex;justify-content:center;flex-wrap:wrap;align-content:flex-start;flex-direction:column}}body.skin-vector .mw-parser-output div.thumb>div:not(.thumbinner){font-size:94%;text-align:center;overflow:hidden;min-width:100px}body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output div.thumb>div:not(.thumbinner){margin:0 auto;max-width:100%!important}</style>
<div class="center">
<div class="thumb tnone">
<div style="display:table;width:650px;">
<div class="thumbcaption" style="display:table-caption;caption-side:top;box-sizing:border-box;"><div style="float: left; margin-top: 0.25em; width: 11px; height: 9.5px;"><div style="position: relative; float: left; width: 0px; height: 0px; -webkit-transform: rotate(360deg); border-style: solid inset; border-width: 9.5px 5.5px 0 5.5px; border-color: #ccc transparent transparent transparent;"></div></div><div style="padding-left: 20px"><big><b>Flowchart of copper refining</b> (Anode casting plant of Uralelektromed)</big>

<li><i><a href="/wiki/Reverberatory_furnace" title="Reverberatory furnace">Reverberatory furnace</a></i></li>

</div>
</div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recycling">Recycling</span></h3>
<p>Like <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> copper is recyclable without any loss of quality, both from raw state and from manufactured products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> In volume, copper is the third most recycled metal after iron and aluminium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> An estimated 80% of all copper ever mined is still in use today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/International_Resource_Panel" title="International Resource Panel">International Resource Panel</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Metal_Stocks_in_Society_report" title="Metal Stocks in Society report">Metal Stocks in Society report</a>, the global per capita stock of copper in use in society is 35–55 kg. Much of this is in more-developed countries (140–300 kg per capita) rather than less-developed countries (30–40 kg per capita).
</p><p>The process of recycling copper is roughly the same as is used to extract copper but requires fewer steps. High-purity scrap copper is melted in a <a href="/wiki/Metallurgical_furnace" title="Metallurgical furnace">furnace</a> and then <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">reduced</a> and cast into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Billet_(semi-finished_product)" title="Billet (semi-finished product)">billets</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ingot" title="Ingot">ingots</a>; lower-purity scrap is refined by <a href="/wiki/Electroplating" title="Electroplating">electroplating</a> in a bath of <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Alloys">Alloys</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1953_Canadian_Dime_in_a_box_of_US_Dimes.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3864" data-file-width="5152" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/1953_Canadian_Dime_in_a_box_of_US_Dimes.jpg/220px-1953_Canadian_Dime_in_a_box_of_US_Dimes.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/1953_Canadian_Dime_in_a_box_of_US_Dimes.jpg/330px-1953_Canadian_Dime_in_a_box_of_US_Dimes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/1953_Canadian_Dime_in_a_box_of_US_Dimes.jpg/440px-1953_Canadian_Dime_in_a_box_of_US_Dimes.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1953_Canadian_Dime_in_a_box_of_US_Dimes.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Copper alloys are widely used in the production of coinage; seen here are two examples - post-1964 American <a href="/wiki/Dime_(United_States_coin)" title="Dime (United States coin)">dimes</a>, which are composed of the alloy <a href="/wiki/Cupronickel" title="Cupronickel">cupronickel</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> and a pre-1968 <a href="/wiki/Dime_(Canadian_coin)" title="Dime (Canadian coin)">Canadian dime</a>, which is composed of an alloy of 80 percent silver and 20 percent copper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_copper_alloys" title="List of copper alloys">List of copper alloys</a></div>
<p>Numerous copper <a href="/wiki/Alloy" title="Alloy">alloys</a> have been formulated, many with important uses. <a href="/wiki/Brass" title="Brass">Brass</a> is an alloy of copper and <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a>. <a href="/wiki/Bronze" title="Bronze">Bronze</a> usually refers to copper-<a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">tin</a> alloys, but can refer to any alloy of copper such as <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_bronze" title="Aluminium bronze">aluminium bronze</a>. Copper is one of the most important constituents of silver and <a href="/wiki/Fineness" title="Fineness">karat</a> gold solders used in the jewelry industry, modifying the color, hardness and melting point of the resulting alloys.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goldalloys_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-goldalloys-47">[47]</a></sup> Some lead-free <a href="/wiki/Solder#Solder_alloys" title="Solder">solders</a> consist of tin alloyed with a small proportion of copper and other metals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>The alloy of copper and <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">nickel</a>, called <a href="/wiki/Cupronickel" title="Cupronickel">cupronickel</a>, is used in low-denomination coins, often for the outer cladding. The US five-cent coin (currently called a <i>nickel</i>) consists of 75% copper and 25% nickel in homogeneous composition. Prior to the introduction of cupronickel, which was widely adopted by countries in the latter half of the 20th century,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> alloys of copper and <a href="/wiki/Silver" title="Silver">silver</a> were also used, with the United States using an alloy of 90% silver and 10% copper until 1965, when circulating silver was removed from all coins with the exception of the Half dollar - these were debased to an alloy of 40% silver and 60% copper between 1965 and 1970.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> The alloy of 90% copper and 10% nickel, remarkable for its resistance to corrosion, is used for various objects exposed to seawater, though it is vulnerable to the sulfides sometimes found in polluted harbors and estuaries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> Alloys of copper with aluminium (about 7%) have a golden color and are used in decorations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emsley_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-emsley-22">[22]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Shakud%C5%8D" title="Shakudō">Shakudō</a></i> is a Japanese decorative alloy of copper containing a low percentage of gold, typically 4–10%, that can be <a href="/wiki/Patina" title="Patina">patinated</a> to a dark blue or black color.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shakudō_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shakudō-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Compounds">Compounds</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CopperIoxide.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="432" data-file-width="344" decoding="async" height="276" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/CopperIoxide.jpg/220px-CopperIoxide.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/CopperIoxide.jpg/330px-CopperIoxide.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/61/CopperIoxide.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CopperIoxide.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A sample of <a href="/wiki/Copper(I)_oxide" title="Copper(I) oxide">copper(I) oxide</a>.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Copper_compounds" title="Category:Copper compounds">Category:Copper compounds</a></div>
<p>Copper forms a rich variety of compounds, usually with <a href="/wiki/Oxidation_state" title="Oxidation state">oxidation states</a> +1 and +2, which are often called <i>cuprous</i> and <i>cupric</i>, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holleman_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Holleman-53">[53]</a></sup> Copper compounds, whether organic <a href="/wiki/Coordination_complex" title="Coordination complex">complexes</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organometallic" title="Organometallic">organometallics</a>, promote or catalyse numerous chemical and biological processes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Binary_compounds">Binary compounds</span></h3>
<p>As with other elements, the simplest compounds of copper are binary compounds, i.e. those containing only two elements, the principal examples being oxides, sulfides, and <a href="/wiki/Halide" title="Halide">halides</a>. Both <a href="/wiki/Copper(I)_oxide" title="Copper(I) oxide">cuprous</a> and <a href="/wiki/Copper(II)_oxide" title="Copper(II) oxide">cupric oxides</a> are known. Among the numerous <a href="/wiki/Copper_sulfide" title="Copper sulfide">copper sulfides</a>, important examples include <a href="/wiki/Copper(I)_sulfide" title="Copper(I) sulfide">copper(I) sulfide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Copper_monosulfide" title="Copper monosulfide">copper(II) sulfide</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Cuprous halides with <a href="/wiki/Copper(I)_fluoride" title="Copper(I) fluoride">fluorine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Copper(I)_chloride" title="Copper(I) chloride">chlorine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Copper(I)_bromide" title="Copper(I) bromide">bromine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Copper(I)_iodide" title="Copper(I) iodide">iodine</a> are known, as are cupric halides with <a href="/wiki/Copper(II)_fluoride" title="Copper(II) fluoride">fluorine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Copper(II)_chloride" title="Copper(II) chloride">chlorine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Copper(II)_bromide" title="Copper(II) bromide">bromine</a>. Attempts to prepare copper(II) iodide yield only copper(I) iodide and iodine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holleman_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-Holleman-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>2 Cu<sup>2+</sup> + 4 I<sup>−</sup> → 2 CuI + I<sub>2</sub></dd></dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coordination_chemistry">Coordination chemistry</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:205px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tetramminkupfer(II)-sulfat-Monohydrat_Kristalle.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="201" data-file-width="203" decoding="async" height="201" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fd/Tetramminkupfer%28II%29-sulfat-Monohydrat_Kristalle.png" width="203"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tetramminkupfer(II)-sulfat-Monohydrat_Kristalle.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Copper(II) gives a deep blue coloration in the presence of ammonia ligands. The one used here is <a href="/wiki/Tetraamminecopper(II)_sulfate" title="Tetraamminecopper(II) sulfate">tetraamminecopper(II) sulfate</a>.</div></div></div><p>
Copper forms <a href="/wiki/Coordination_complex" title="Coordination complex">coordination complexes</a> with <a href="/wiki/Ligand" title="Ligand">ligands</a>. In aqueous solution, copper(II) exists as <span class="chemf nowrap">[Cu(H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span>]<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span>. This complex exhibits the fastest water exchange rate (speed of water ligands attaching and detaching) for any transition <a href="/wiki/Metal_aquo_complex" title="Metal aquo complex">metal aquo complex</a>. Adding aqueous <a href="/wiki/Sodium_hydroxide" title="Sodium hydroxide">sodium hydroxide</a> causes the precipitation of light blue solid <a href="/wiki/Copper(II)_hydroxide" title="Copper(II) hydroxide">copper(II) hydroxide</a>. A simplified equation is: </p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cu-pourbaix-diagram.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="420" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Cu-pourbaix-diagram.svg/220px-Cu-pourbaix-diagram.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Cu-pourbaix-diagram.svg/330px-Cu-pourbaix-diagram.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Cu-pourbaix-diagram.svg/440px-Cu-pourbaix-diagram.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cu-pourbaix-diagram.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pourbaix diagram for copper in uncomplexed media (anions other than OH- not considered). Ion concentration 0.001 m (mol/kg water). Temperature 25 °C.</div></div></div>
<dl><dd>Cu<sup>2+</sup> + 2 OH<sup>−</sup> → Cu(OH)<sub>2</sub></dd></dl>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ammonia_solution" title="Ammonia solution">Aqueous ammonia</a> results in the same precipitate. Upon adding excess ammonia, the precipitate dissolves, forming <a href="/wiki/Schweizer%27s_reagent" title="Schweizer's reagent">tetraamminecopper(II)</a>:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="chemf nowrap">Cu(H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>(OH)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span> + 4 NH<sub>3</sub> → <span class="chemf nowrap">[Cu(H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>(NH<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">3</sub></span>)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">4</sub></span>]<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2+</sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sub></span></span> + 2 H<sub>2</sub>O + 2 OH<sup>−</sup></dd></dl>
<p>Many other <a href="/wiki/Oxyanion" title="Oxyanion">oxyanions</a> form complexes; these include <a href="/wiki/Copper(II)_acetate" title="Copper(II) acetate">copper(II) acetate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Copper(II)_nitrate" title="Copper(II) nitrate">copper(II) nitrate</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Copper(II)_carbonate" title="Copper(II) carbonate">copper(II) carbonate</a>. <a href="/wiki/Copper(II)_sulfate" title="Copper(II) sulfate">Copper(II) sulfate</a> forms a blue crystalline penta<a href="/wiki/Hydrate" title="Hydrate">hydrate</a>, the most familiar copper compound in the laboratory. It is used in a <a href="/wiki/Fungicide" title="Fungicide">fungicide</a> called the <a href="/wiki/Bordeaux_mixture" title="Bordeaux mixture">Bordeaux mixture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boux_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boux-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tetraamminediaquacopper(II)-3D-balls.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1100" data-file-width="1100" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Tetraamminediaquacopper%28II%29-3D-balls.png/200px-Tetraamminediaquacopper%28II%29-3D-balls.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Tetraamminediaquacopper%28II%29-3D-balls.png/300px-Tetraamminediaquacopper%28II%29-3D-balls.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Tetraamminediaquacopper%28II%29-3D-balls.png/400px-Tetraamminediaquacopper%28II%29-3D-balls.png 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tetraamminediaquacopper(II)-3D-balls.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ball-and-stick_model" title="Ball-and-stick model">Ball-and-stick model</a> of the complex [Cu(NH<sub>3</sub>)<sub>4</sub>(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub>2</sub>]<sup>2+</sup>, illustrating the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Octahedral_coordination_geometry" title="Octahedral coordination geometry">octahedral coordination geometry</a> common for copper(II).</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Polyol" title="Polyol">Polyols</a>, compounds containing more than one alcohol <a href="/wiki/Functional_group" title="Functional group">functional group</a>, generally interact with cupric salts. For example, copper salts are used to test for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reducing_sugars" title="Reducing sugars">reducing sugars</a>. Specifically, using <a href="/wiki/Benedict%27s_reagent" title="Benedict's reagent">Benedict's reagent</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fehling%27s_solution" title="Fehling's solution">Fehling's solution</a> the presence of the sugar is signaled by a color change from blue Cu(II) to reddish copper(I) oxide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Schweizer's reagent and related complexes with <a href="/wiki/Ethylenediamine" title="Ethylenediamine">ethylenediamine</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Amine" title="Amine">amines</a> dissolve <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">cellulose</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Amino_acid" title="Amino acid">Amino acids</a> form very stable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chelate_complex" title="Chelate complex">chelate complexes</a> with copper(II).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> Many wet-chemical tests for copper ions exist, one involving <a href="/wiki/Potassium_ferrocyanide" title="Potassium ferrocyanide">potassium ferrocyanide</a>, which gives a brown precipitate with copper(II) salts.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Organocopper_chemistry">Organocopper chemistry</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Organocopper_compound" title="Organocopper compound">Organocopper compound</a></div>
<p>Compounds that contain a carbon-copper bond are known as organocopper compounds. They are very reactive towards oxygen to form copper(I) oxide and have <a href="/wiki/Reactions_of_organocopper_reagents" title="Reactions of organocopper reagents">many uses in chemistry</a>. They are synthesized by treating copper(I) compounds with <a href="/wiki/Grignard_reaction" title="Grignard reaction">Grignard reagents</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Terminal_alkyne" title="Terminal alkyne">terminal alkynes</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organolithium_compound" title="Organolithium compound">organolithium reagents</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> in particular, the last reaction described produces a <a href="/wiki/Gilman_reagent" title="Gilman reagent">Gilman reagent</a>. These can undergo <a href="/wiki/Substitution_reaction" title="Substitution reaction">substitution</a> with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alkyl_halides" title="Alkyl halides">alkyl halides</a> to form <a href="/wiki/Coupling_reaction" title="Coupling reaction">coupling products</a>; as such, they are important in the field of <a href="/wiki/Organic_synthesis" title="Organic synthesis">organic synthesis</a>. <a href="/wiki/Copper(I)_acetylide" title="Copper(I) acetylide">Copper(I) acetylide</a> is highly shock-sensitive but is an intermediate in reactions such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cadiot-Chodkiewicz_coupling" title="Cadiot-Chodkiewicz coupling">Cadiot-Chodkiewicz coupling</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Sonogashira_coupling" title="Sonogashira coupling">Sonogashira coupling</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nucleophilic_conjugate_addition" title="Nucleophilic conjugate addition">Conjugate addition</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alpha-beta_Unsaturated_carbonyl_compounds" title="Alpha-beta Unsaturated carbonyl compounds">enones</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Carbometalation" title="Carbometalation">carbocupration</a> of alkynes<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> can also be achieved with organocopper compounds. Copper(I) forms a variety of weak complexes with <a href="/wiki/Alkene" title="Alkene">alkenes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a>, especially in the presence of amine ligands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Copper.28III.29_and_copper.28IV.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Copper(III)_and_copper(IV)">Copper(III) and copper(IV)</span></h3>
<p>Copper(III) is most often found in oxides. A simple example is potassium <a href="/wiki/Cuprate" title="Cuprate">cuprate</a>, KCuO<sub>2</sub>, a blue-black solid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> The most extensively studied copper(III) compounds are the <a href="/wiki/Cuprate_superconductor" title="Cuprate superconductor">cuprate superconductors</a>. <a href="/wiki/Yttrium_barium_copper_oxide" title="Yttrium barium copper oxide">Yttrium barium copper oxide</a> (YBa<sub>2</sub>Cu<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>) consists of both Cu(II) and Cu(III) centres. Like oxide, <a href="/wiki/Fluoride" title="Fluoride">fluoride</a> is a highly <a href="/wiki/Base_(chemistry)" title="Base (chemistry)">basic</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anion" title="Anion">anion</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> and is known to stabilize metal ions in high oxidation states. Both copper(III) and even copper(IV) fluorides are known, <a href="/wiki/Potassium_hexafluorocuprate(III)" title="Potassium hexafluorocuprate(III)">K<sub>3</sub>CuF<sub>6</sub></a> and <a href="/wiki/Caesium_hexafluorocuprate(IV)" title="Caesium hexafluorocuprate(IV)">Cs<sub>2</sub>CuF<sub>6</sub></a>, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Holleman_53-2"><a href="#cite_note-Holleman-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some copper proteins form <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxo_complex" title="Oxo complex">oxo complexes</a>, which also feature copper(III).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> With <a href="/wiki/Tetrapeptide" title="Tetrapeptide">tetrapeptides</a>, purple-colored copper(III) complexes are stabilized by the deprotonated <a href="/wiki/Amide" title="Amide">amide</a> ligands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>Complexes of copper(III) are also found as intermediates in reactions of organocopper compounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> For example, in the <a href="/wiki/Kharasch%E2%80%93Sosnovsky_reaction" title="Kharasch–Sosnovsky reaction">Kharasch–Sosnovsky reaction</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<p>A timeline of copper illustrates how this metal has advanced human civilization for the past 11,000 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistoric">Prehistoric</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Copper_Age">Copper Age</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Copper_Age" title="Copper Age">Copper Age</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Minoan_copper_ingot_from_Zakros,_Crete.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="803" data-file-width="1264" decoding="async" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Minoan_copper_ingot_from_Zakros%2C_Crete.jpg/220px-Minoan_copper_ingot_from_Zakros%2C_Crete.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Minoan_copper_ingot_from_Zakros%2C_Crete.jpg/330px-Minoan_copper_ingot_from_Zakros%2C_Crete.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Minoan_copper_ingot_from_Zakros%2C_Crete.jpg/440px-Minoan_copper_ingot_from_Zakros%2C_Crete.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Minoan_copper_ingot_from_Zakros,_Crete.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A corroded copper <a href="/wiki/Ingot" title="Ingot">ingot</a> from <a href="/wiki/Zakros" title="Zakros">Zakros</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a>, shaped in the form of an animal skin (<a href="/wiki/Oxhide_ingot" title="Oxhide ingot">oxhide</a>) typical in that era.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ReconstructedOetziAxe.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3126" data-file-width="1980" decoding="async" height="347" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/ReconstructedOetziAxe.jpg/220px-ReconstructedOetziAxe.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/ReconstructedOetziAxe.jpg/330px-ReconstructedOetziAxe.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/ReconstructedOetziAxe.jpg/440px-ReconstructedOetziAxe.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ReconstructedOetziAxe.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Many tools during the <a href="/wiki/Chalcolithic" title="Chalcolithic">Chalcolithic</a> Era included copper, such as the blade of this replica of <a href="/wiki/%C3%96tzi" title="Ötzi">Ötzi</a>'s axe</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chrysocolla_Timna_070613.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1540" data-file-width="2264" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Chrysocolla_Timna_070613.jpg/220px-Chrysocolla_Timna_070613.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Chrysocolla_Timna_070613.jpg/330px-Chrysocolla_Timna_070613.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Chrysocolla_Timna_070613.jpg/440px-Chrysocolla_Timna_070613.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chrysocolla_Timna_070613.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Copper ore (<a href="/wiki/Chrysocolla" title="Chrysocolla">chrysocolla</a>) in <a href="/wiki/Cambrian" title="Cambrian">Cambrian</a> sandstone from <a href="/wiki/Chalcolithic" title="Chalcolithic">Chalcolithic</a> mines in the <a href="/wiki/Timna_Valley" title="Timna Valley">Timna Valley</a>, southern <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Copper occurs naturally as <a href="/wiki/Native_copper" title="Native copper">native metallic copper</a> and was known to some of the oldest civilizations on record. The history of copper use dates to 9000 BC in the Middle East;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-discovery_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-discovery-73">[73]</a></sup> a copper pendant was found in northern Iraq that dates to 8700 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Evidence suggests that gold and <a href="/wiki/Meteoric_iron" title="Meteoric iron">meteoric iron</a> (but not smelted iron) were the only metals used by humans before copper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vander_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-vander-75">[75]</a></sup> The history of copper metallurgy is thought to follow this sequence: First, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cold_forming" title="Cold forming">cold working</a> of native copper, then <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Annealing_(metallurgy)" title="Annealing (metallurgy)">annealing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Smelting" title="Smelting">smelting</a>, and, finally, <a href="/wiki/Lost-wax_casting" title="Lost-wax casting">lost-wax casting</a>. In southeastern <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a>, all four of these techniques appear more or less simultaneously at the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> c. 7500 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Renfrew1990_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Renfrew1990-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>Copper smelting was independently invented in different places. It was probably discovered in China before 2800 BC, in Central America around 600 AD, and in West Africa about the 9th or 10th century AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Investment_casting" title="Investment casting">Investment casting</a> was invented in 4500–4000 BC in Southeast Asia<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-discovery_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-discovery-73">[73]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_dating" title="Carbon dating">carbon dating</a> has established mining at <a href="/wiki/Alderley_Edge_Mines" title="Alderley Edge Mines">Alderley Edge</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cheshire" title="Cheshire">Cheshire</a>, UK, at 2280 to 1890 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C3%96tzi_the_Iceman" title="Ötzi the Iceman">Ötzi the Iceman</a>, a male dated from 3300 to 3200 BC, was found with an axe with a copper head 99.7% pure; high levels of <a href="/wiki/Arsenic" title="Arsenic">arsenic</a> in his hair suggest an involvement in copper smelting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CSA_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-CSA-79">[79]</a></sup> Experience with copper has assisted the development of other metals; in particular, copper smelting led to the discovery of <a href="/wiki/Bloomery" title="Bloomery">iron smelting</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CSA_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-CSA-79">[79]</a></sup> Production in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Copper_Complex" title="Old Copper Complex">Old Copper Complex</a> in Michigan and Wisconsin is dated between 6000 and 3000 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-occ_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-occ-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> Natural bronze, a type of copper made from ores rich in silicon, arsenic, and (rarely) tin, came into general use in the Balkans around 5500 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Bronze_Age">Bronze Age</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a></div>
<p>Alloying copper with tin to make bronze was first practiced about 4000 years after the discovery of copper smelting, and about 2000 years after "natural bronze" had come into general use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> Bronze artifacts from the <a href="/wiki/Vin%C4%8Da_culture" title="Vinča culture">Vinča culture</a> date to 4500 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-antiquity1312_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-antiquity1312-84">[84]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumerian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Egyptian</a> artifacts of copper and bronze alloys date to 3000 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hist_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-hist-85">[85]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> began in Southeastern Europe around 3700–3300 BC, in Northwestern Europe about 2500 BC. It ended with the beginning of the Iron Age, 2000–1000 BC in the Near East, and 600 BC in Northern Europe. The transition between the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> period and the Bronze Age was formerly termed the <a href="/wiki/Chalcolithic" title="Chalcolithic">Chalcolithic</a> period (copper-stone), when copper tools were used with stone tools. The term has gradually fallen out of favor because in some parts of the world, the Chalcolithic and Neolithic are coterminous at both ends. Brass, an alloy of copper and zinc, is of much more recent origin. It was known to the Greeks, but became a significant supplement to bronze during the Roman Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hist_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-hist-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_and_post-classical">Ancient and post-classical</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:102px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Venus_symbol_(fixed_width).svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="15" data-file-width="15" decoding="async" height="100" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Venus_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg/100px-Venus_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Venus_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg/150px-Venus_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Venus_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg/200px-Venus_symbol_%28fixed_width%29.svg.png 2x" width="100"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Venus_symbol_(fixed_width).svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In <a href="/wiki/Alchemy" title="Alchemy">alchemy</a> the symbol for copper was also the symbol for the goddess and planet <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a>.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TimnaChalcolithicMine.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/TimnaChalcolithicMine.JPG/220px-TimnaChalcolithicMine.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/TimnaChalcolithicMine.JPG/330px-TimnaChalcolithicMine.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/TimnaChalcolithicMine.JPG/440px-TimnaChalcolithicMine.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TimnaChalcolithicMine.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chalcolithic copper mine in <a href="/wiki/Timna_Valley" title="Timna Valley">Timna Valley</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Negev_Desert" title="Negev Desert">Negev Desert</a>, Israel.</div></div></div>
<p>In Greece, copper was known by the name <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language romanization"><i lang="grc-Latn">chalkos</i></span> (χαλκός). It was an important resource for the Romans, Greeks and other ancient peoples. In Roman times, it was known as <i>aes Cyprium</i>, <i>aes</i> being the generic Latin term for copper alloys and <i>Cyprium</i> from <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a>, where much copper was mined. The phrase was simplified to <i>cuprum</i>, hence the English <i>copper</i>. <a href="/wiki/Aphrodite" title="Aphrodite">Aphrodite</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Venus_(goddess)" title="Venus (goddess)">Venus</a> in Rome) represented copper in mythology and alchemy because of its lustrous beauty and its ancient use in producing mirrors; Cyprus, the source of copper, was sacred to the goddess. The seven heavenly bodies known to the ancients were associated with the seven metals known in antiquity, and Venus was assigned to copper, both because of the connection to the goddess and because Venus was the brightest heavenly body after the Sun and Moon and so corresponded to the most lustrous and desirable metal after gold and silver.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p><p>Copper was first mined in ancient Britain as early as 2100 BC. Mining at the largest of these mines, the <a href="/wiki/Great_Orme" title="Great Orme">Great Orme</a>, continued into the late Bronze Age. Mining seems to have been largely restricted to <a href="/wiki/Supergene_(geology)" title="Supergene (geology)">supergene</a> ores, which were easier to smelt. The rich copper deposits of <a href="/wiki/Cornwall" title="Cornwall">Cornwall</a> seem to have been largely untouched, in spite of extensive <a href="/wiki/Tin" title="Tin">tin</a> mining in the region, for reasons likely social and political rather than technological.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>In North America, copper mining began with marginal workings by Native Americans. Native copper is known to have been extracted from sites on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isle_Royale" title="Isle Royale">Isle Royale</a> with primitive stone tools between 800 and 1600.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> Copper metallurgy was flourishing in South America, particularly in Peru around 1000 AD. Copper burial ornamentals from the 15th century have been uncovered, but the metal's commercial production did not start until the early 20th century.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The cultural role of copper has been important, particularly in currency. Romans in the 6th through 3rd centuries BC used copper lumps as money. At first, the copper itself was valued, but gradually the shape and look of the copper became more important. <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Julius Caesar</a> had his own coins made from brass, while <a href="/wiki/Augustus" title="Augustus">Octavianus Augustus Caesar</a>'s coins were made from Cu-Pb-Sn alloys. With an estimated annual output of around 15,000 t, <a href="/wiki/Roman_metallurgy" title="Roman metallurgy">Roman copper mining and smelting activities</a> reached a scale unsurpassed until the time of the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Roman_province" title="Roman province">provinces</a> most intensely mined were those of <a href="/wiki/Hispania" title="Hispania">Hispania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a> and in Central Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p><p>The gates of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temple_of_Jerusalem" title="Temple of Jerusalem">Temple of Jerusalem</a> used <a href="/wiki/Corinthian_bronze" title="Corinthian bronze">Corinthian bronze</a> treated with <a href="/wiki/Depletion_gilding" title="Depletion gilding">depletion gilding</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="Bronze is not a gold alloy, but depletion gilding can be done only on gold alloy. (June 2016)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The process was most prevalent in <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a>, where alchemy is thought to have begun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> In ancient <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, copper was used in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Holistic" title="Holistic">holistic</a> medical science <a href="/wiki/Ayurveda" title="Ayurveda">Ayurveda</a> for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Surgical" title="Surgical">surgical</a> instruments and other medical equipment. <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egyptians</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Kingdom" title="Old Kingdom">~2400 BC</a>) used copper for sterilizing wounds and drinking water, and later to treat headaches, burns, and itching.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
