<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Copper_Ornaments.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3198" data-file-width="2132" decoding="async" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Copper_Ornaments.jpg/220px-Copper_Ornaments.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Copper_Ornaments.jpg/330px-Copper_Ornaments.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Copper_Ornaments.jpg/440px-Copper_Ornaments.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Copper_Ornaments.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Copper Ornaments</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern">Modern</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AngleseyCopperStream.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="656" data-file-width="492" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/AngleseyCopperStream.jpg/220px-AngleseyCopperStream.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/AngleseyCopperStream.jpg/330px-AngleseyCopperStream.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/AngleseyCopperStream.jpg/440px-AngleseyCopperStream.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AngleseyCopperStream.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Acid_mine_drainage" title="Acid mine drainage">Acid mine drainage</a> affecting the stream running from the disused <a href="/wiki/Parys_Mountain" title="Parys Mountain">Parys Mountain</a> copper mines</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Copper_Pot.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3608" data-file-width="3850" decoding="async" height="206" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Copper_Pot.jpg/220px-Copper_Pot.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Copper_Pot.jpg/330px-Copper_Pot.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Copper_Pot.jpg/440px-Copper_Pot.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Copper_Pot.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>18th-century copper <a href="/wiki/Kettle" title="Kettle">kettle</a> from Norway made from Swedish copper</div></div></div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Copper_Mountain" title="Great Copper Mountain">Great Copper Mountain</a> was a mine in Falun, Sweden, that operated from the 10th century to 1992. It satisfied two-thirds of Europe's copper consumption in the 17th century and helped fund many of Sweden's wars during that time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> It was referred to as the nation's treasury; Sweden had a <a href="/wiki/History_of_copper_currency_in_Sweden" title="History of copper currency in Sweden">copper backed currency</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Viipuri_-_Viborg.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2376" data-file-width="4098" decoding="async" height="128" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Viipuri_-_Viborg.jpg/220px-Viipuri_-_Viborg.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Viipuri_-_Viborg.jpg/330px-Viipuri_-_Viborg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Viipuri_-_Viborg.jpg/440px-Viipuri_-_Viborg.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Viipuri_-_Viborg.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Chalcography" title="Chalcography">Chalcography</a> of the city of <a href="/wiki/Vyborg" title="Vyborg">Vyborg</a> at the turn of the 17th and 18th centuries. The year 1709 carved on the printing plate.</div></div></div>
<p>Copper is used in roofing,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-15">[15]</a></sup> currency, and for photographic technology known as the <a href="/wiki/Daguerreotype" title="Daguerreotype">daguerreotype</a>. Copper was used in <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> sculpture, and was used to construct the <a href="/wiki/Statue_of_Liberty" title="Statue of Liberty">Statue of Liberty</a>; copper continues to be used in construction of various types. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Copper_plating" title="Copper plating">Copper plating</a> and <a href="/wiki/Copper_sheathing" title="Copper sheathing">copper sheathing</a> were widely used to protect the under-water hulls of ships, a technique pioneered by the British Admiralty in the 18th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norddeutsche_Affinerie" title="Norddeutsche Affinerie">Norddeutsche Affinerie</a> in Hamburg was the first modern <a href="/wiki/Electroplating" title="Electroplating">electroplating</a> plant, starting its production in 1876.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> The German scientist <a href="/wiki/Gottfried_Osann" title="Gottfried Osann">Gottfried Osann</a> invented <a href="/wiki/Powder_metallurgy" title="Powder metallurgy">powder metallurgy</a> in 1830 while determining the metal's atomic mass; around then it was discovered that the amount and type of alloying element (e.g., tin) to copper would affect bell tones.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>During the rise in demand for copper for the Age of Electricity, from the 1880s until the Great Depression of the 1930s, the United States produced one third to half the world's newly mined copper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> Major districts included the Keweenaw district of northern Michigan, primarily native copper deposits, which was eclipsed by the vast sulphide deposits of <a href="/wiki/Butte,_Montana" title="Butte, Montana">Butte, Montana</a> in the late 1880s, which itself was eclipsed by porphyry deposits of the Souhwest United States, especially at <a href="/wiki/Bingham_Canyon,_Utah" title="Bingham Canyon, Utah">Bingham Canyon, Utah</a> and <a href="/wiki/Morenci,_Arizona" title="Morenci, Arizona">Morenci, Arizona</a>. Introduction of open pit steam shovel mining and innovations in smelting, refining, flotation concentration and other processing steps led to mass production. Early in the twentieth century, <a href="/wiki/Arizona" title="Arizona">Arizona</a> ranked first, followed by <a href="/wiki/Montana" title="Montana">Montana</a>, then <a href="/wiki/Utah" title="Utah">Utah</a> and <a href="/wiki/Michigan" title="Michigan">Michigan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Flash_smelting" title="Flash smelting">Flash smelting</a> was developed by <a href="/wiki/Outokumpu" title="Outokumpu">Outokumpu</a> in Finland and first applied at <a href="/wiki/Harjavalta" title="Harjavalta">Harjavalta</a> in 1949; the energy-efficient process accounts for 50% of the world's primary copper production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_Council_of_Copper_Exporting_Countries" title="Intergovernmental Council of Copper Exporting Countries">Intergovernmental Council of Copper Exporting Countries</a>, formed in 1967 by Chile, Peru, Zaire and Zambia, operated in the copper market as <a href="/wiki/OPEC" title="OPEC">OPEC</a> does in oil, though it never achieved the same influence, particularly because the second-largest producer, the United States, was never a member; it was dissolved in 1988.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Copper_in_renewable_energy" title="Copper in renewable energy">Copper in renewable energy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kupferfittings_4062.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Kupferfittings_4062.jpg/220px-Kupferfittings_4062.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Kupferfittings_4062.jpg/330px-Kupferfittings_4062.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Kupferfittings_4062.jpg/440px-Kupferfittings_4062.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kupferfittings_4062.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Copper fittings for soldered plumbing joints</div></div></div>
<p>The major applications of copper are electrical wire (60%), roofing and plumbing (20%), and industrial machinery (15%). Copper is used mostly as a pure metal, but when greater hardness is required, it is put into such alloys as <a href="/wiki/Brass" title="Brass">brass</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bronze" title="Bronze">bronze</a> (5% of total use).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emsley_22-3"><a href="#cite_note-emsley-22">[22]</a></sup> For more than two centuries, copper paint has been used on boat hulls to control the growth of plants and shellfish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> A small part of the copper supply is used for nutritional supplements and fungicides in agriculture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boux_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-Boux-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Applications_for_Copper_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Applications_for_Copper-101">[101]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Machining" title="Machining">Machining</a> of copper is possible, although alloys are preferred for good <a href="/wiki/Machinability" title="Machinability">machinability</a> in creating intricate parts.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wire_and_cable">Wire and cable</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Copper_wire_and_cable" title="Copper wire and cable">Copper wire and cable</a></div>
<p>Despite competition from other materials, copper remains the preferred <a href="/wiki/Electrical_conductor" title="Electrical conductor">electrical conductor</a> in nearly all categories of electrical wiring except overhead <a href="/wiki/Electric_power_transmission" title="Electric power transmission">electric power transmission</a> where <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a> is often preferred.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> Copper wire is used in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Power_generation" title="Power generation">power generation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Power_transmission" title="Power transmission">power transmission</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Power_distribution" title="Power distribution">power distribution</a>, <a href="/wiki/Telecommunications" title="Telecommunications">telecommunications</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electronics" title="Electronics">electronics</a> circuitry, and countless types of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_equipment" title="Electrical equipment">electrical equipment</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Electrical_wiring" title="Electrical wiring">Electrical wiring</a> is the most important market for the copper industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> This includes structural power wiring, power distribution cable, appliance wire, communications cable, automotive wire and cable, and magnet wire. Roughly half of all copper mined is used for electrical wire and cable conductors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> Many electrical devices rely on copper wiring because of its multitude of inherent beneficial properties, such as its high <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_conductivity" title="Electrical conductivity">electrical conductivity</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tensile_strength" title="Tensile strength">tensile strength</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ductility" title="Ductility">ductility</a>, <a href="/wiki/Creep_(deformation)" title="Creep (deformation)">creep (deformation)</a> resistance, <a href="/wiki/Corrosion" title="Corrosion">corrosion</a> resistance, low <a href="/wiki/Thermal_expansion" title="Thermal expansion">thermal expansion</a>, high <a href="/wiki/Thermal_conductivity" title="Thermal conductivity">thermal conductivity</a>, ease of <a href="/wiki/Solder" title="Solder">soldering</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Malleability" title="Malleability">malleability</a>, and ease of installation.
</p><p>For a short period from the late 1960s to the late 1970s, copper wiring was replaced by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aluminium_wiring" title="Aluminium wiring">aluminium wiring</a> in many housing construction projects in America. The new wiring was implicated in a number of house fires and the industry returned to copper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Electronics_and_related_devices">Electronics and related devices</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Busbars.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="275" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Busbars.jpg/220px-Busbars.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Busbars.jpg/330px-Busbars.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Busbars.jpg/440px-Busbars.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Busbars.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Copper electrical <a href="/wiki/Busbar" title="Busbar">busbars</a> distributing power to a large building</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Integrated_circuit" title="Integrated circuit">Integrated circuits</a> and <a href="/wiki/Printed_circuit_board" title="Printed circuit board">printed circuit boards</a> increasingly feature copper in place of aluminium because of its superior electrical conductivity; <a href="/wiki/Heat_sink" title="Heat sink">heat sinks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Heat_exchanger" title="Heat exchanger">heat exchangers</a> use copper because of its superior heat dissipation properties. <a href="/wiki/Electromagnet" title="Electromagnet">Electromagnets</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_tube" title="Vacuum tube">vacuum tubes</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cathode_ray_tube" title="Cathode ray tube">cathode ray tubes</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetron" title="Magnetron">magnetrons</a> in microwave ovens use copper, as do <a href="/wiki/Waveguide" title="Waveguide">waveguides</a> for microwave radiation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Electric_motors">Electric motors</span></h3>
<p>Copper's superior <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Copper_wire_and_cable#Electrical_conductivity" title="Copper wire and cable">conductivity</a> enhances the efficiency of electrical <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Motor_(device)" title="Motor (device)">motors</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> This is important because motors and motor-driven systems account for 43%–46% of all global electricity consumption and 69% of all electricity used by industry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> Increasing the mass and cross section of copper in a <a href="/wiki/Inductor" title="Inductor">coil</a> increases the efficiency of the motor. <a href="/wiki/Induction_motor" title="Induction motor">Copper motor rotors</a>, a new technology designed for motor applications where energy savings are prime design objectives,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> are enabling general-purpose <a href="/wiki/Induction_motor" title="Induction motor">induction motors</a> to meet and exceed <a href="/wiki/National_Electrical_Manufacturers_Association" title="National Electrical Manufacturers Association">National Electrical Manufacturers Association</a> (NEMA) <a href="/wiki/Premium_efficiency" title="Premium efficiency">premium efficiency</a> standards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Renewable_energy_production">Renewable energy production</span></h3>
<div class="excerpt-block"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066933788">.mw-parser-output .excerpt-hat .mw-editsection-like{font-style:normal}</style><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable dablink excerpt-hat selfref" role="note">This section is an excerpt from <a href="/wiki/Copper_in_renewable_energy" title="Copper in renewable energy">Copper in renewable energy</a>.<span class="mw-editsection-like plainlinks"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Copper_in_renewable_energy&amp;action=edit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div><div class="excerpt">
<p><a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">Renewable energy</a> sources such as <a href="/wiki/Solar_energy" title="Solar energy">solar</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wind_energy" title="Wind energy">wind</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tidal_power" title="Tidal power">tidal</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydroelectric_power" title="Hydroelectric power">hydro</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biomass_energy" title="Biomass energy">biomass</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Geothermal_energy" title="Geothermal energy">geothermal</a> have become significant sectors of the energy market.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> The rapid growth of these sources in the 21st century has been prompted by increasing costs of <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuels</a> as well as their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environmental_impact" title="Environmental impact">environmental impact</a> issues that <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel_phase-out" title="Fossil fuel phase-out">significantly lowered</a> their use.
</p><p>Copper plays an important role in these renewable energy systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> Copper usage averages up to five times more in renewable energy systems than in traditional power generation, such as fossil fuel and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power" title="Nuclear power">nuclear</a> power plants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Copper_in_renewable_energy_issues.solarindustrymag.com_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-Copper_in_renewable_energy_issues.solarindustrymag.com-121">[121]</a></sup> Since copper is an excellent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thermal_conductor" title="Thermal conductor">thermal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Electrical_conductor" title="Electrical conductor">electrical conductor</a> among engineering metals (second only to silver),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> electrical systems that utilize copper generate and transmit energy with high efficiency and with minimum environmental impacts.
</p><p>When choosing electrical conductors, facility planners and engineers factor capital investment costs of materials against operational savings due to their electrical energy efficiencies over their useful lives, plus maintenance costs. Copper often fares well in these calculations. A factor called "copper usage intensity,” is a measure of the quantity of copper necessary to install one megawatt of new power-generating capacity.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Residuo_electronico_colombia_cobre_reciclaje_metales_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1800" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="270" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Residuo_electronico_colombia_cobre_reciclaje_metales_2.jpg/180px-Residuo_electronico_colombia_cobre_reciclaje_metales_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Residuo_electronico_colombia_cobre_reciclaje_metales_2.jpg/270px-Residuo_electronico_colombia_cobre_reciclaje_metales_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Residuo_electronico_colombia_cobre_reciclaje_metales_2.jpg/360px-Residuo_electronico_colombia_cobre_reciclaje_metales_2.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Residuo_electronico_colombia_cobre_reciclaje_metales_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Copper wires for recycling</div></div></div>
<p>When planning for a new renewable power facility, engineers and product specifiers seek to avoid supply shortages of selected materials. According to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Geological_Survey" title="United States Geological Survey">United States Geological Survey</a>, in-ground copper <a href="/wiki/Mineral_resource_classification" title="Mineral resource classification">reserves</a> have increased more than 700% since 1950, from almost 100 million tonnes to 720 million tonnes in 2017, despite the fact that world refined usage has more than tripled in the last 50 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> Copper resources are estimated to exceed 5,000 million tonnes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup>
</p><p>Bolstering the supply from <a href="/wiki/Copper_extraction" title="Copper extraction">copper extraction</a> is the fact that more than 30 percent of copper installed during the last decade came from recycled sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> Its <a href="/wiki/Recycling_by_material" title="Recycling by material">recycling rate</a> is higher than any other metal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup>
</p>
This article discusses the role of copper in various renewable energy generation systems.</div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">Architecture</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Copper_in_architecture" title="Copper in architecture">Copper in architecture</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Minneapolis_City_Hall.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2280" data-file-width="3028" decoding="async" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Minneapolis_City_Hall.jpg/220px-Minneapolis_City_Hall.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Minneapolis_City_Hall.jpg/330px-Minneapolis_City_Hall.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Minneapolis_City_Hall.jpg/440px-Minneapolis_City_Hall.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Minneapolis_City_Hall.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Copper roof on the <a href="/wiki/Minneapolis_City_Hall" title="Minneapolis City Hall">Minneapolis City Hall</a>, coated with <a href="/wiki/Patina" title="Patina">patina</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Copper_utensils_Jerusalem.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="750" data-file-width="488" decoding="async" height="338" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Copper_utensils_Jerusalem.jpg/220px-Copper_utensils_Jerusalem.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Copper_utensils_Jerusalem.jpg/330px-Copper_utensils_Jerusalem.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Copper_utensils_Jerusalem.jpg/440px-Copper_utensils_Jerusalem.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Copper_utensils_Jerusalem.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Old copper utensils in a Jerusalem restaurant</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Large_copper_bowl._Dhankar_Gompa.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1728" data-file-width="2304" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Large_copper_bowl._Dhankar_Gompa.jpg/220px-Large_copper_bowl._Dhankar_Gompa.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Large_copper_bowl._Dhankar_Gompa.jpg/330px-Large_copper_bowl._Dhankar_Gompa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Large_copper_bowl._Dhankar_Gompa.jpg/440px-Large_copper_bowl._Dhankar_Gompa.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Large_copper_bowl._Dhankar_Gompa.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Large copper bowl. <a href="/wiki/Dhankar_Gompa" title="Dhankar Gompa">Dhankar Gompa</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Copper has been used since ancient times as a durable, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corrosion_resistance" title="Corrosion resistance">corrosion resistant</a>, and weatherproof architectural material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roofing_material" title="Roofing material">Roofs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flashing_(weatherproofing)" title="Flashing (weatherproofing)">flashings</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rain_gutter" title="Rain gutter">rain gutters</a>, <a href="/wiki/Downspout" title="Downspout">downspouts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dome" title="Dome">domes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spire" title="Spire">spires</a>, vaults, and <a href="/wiki/Door" title="Door">doors</a> have been made from copper for hundreds or thousands of years. Copper's architectural use has been expanded in modern times to include interior and exterior <a href="/wiki/Copper_in_architecture#Wall_cladding" title="Copper in architecture">wall cladding</a>, building <a href="/wiki/Expansion_joint" title="Expansion joint">expansion joints</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/RF_shielding" title="RF shielding">radio frequency shielding</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Antimicrobial_copper-alloy_touch_surfaces" title="Antimicrobial copper-alloy touch surfaces">antimicrobial</a> and decorative indoor products such as attractive handrails, bathroom fixtures, and counter tops. Some of copper's other important benefits as an architectural material include low <a href="/wiki/Thermal_expansion" title="Thermal expansion">thermal movement</a>, light weight, <a href="/wiki/Lightning_rod" title="Lightning rod">lightning protection</a>, and recyclability
</p><p>The metal's distinctive natural green <a href="/wiki/Patina" title="Patina">patina</a> has long been coveted by architects and designers. The final patina is a particularly durable layer that is highly resistant to atmospheric corrosion, thereby protecting the underlying metal against further weathering.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> It can be a mixture of carbonate and sulfate compounds in various amounts, depending upon environmental conditions such as sulfur-containing acid rain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> Architectural copper and its alloys can also be <a href="/wiki/Copper_in_architecture#Finishes" title="Copper in architecture">'finished'</a> to take on a particular look, feel, or color. Finishes include mechanical surface treatments, chemical coloring, and coatings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup>
</p><p>Copper has excellent <a href="/wiki/Brazing" title="Brazing">brazing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Soldering" title="Soldering">soldering</a> properties and can be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Welded" title="Welded">welded</a>; the best results are obtained with <a href="/wiki/Gas_metal_arc_welding" title="Gas metal arc welding">gas metal arc welding</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Antibiofouling">Antibiofouling</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Copper_alloys_in_aquaculture" title="Copper alloys in aquaculture">Copper alloys in aquaculture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Copper_sheathing" title="Copper sheathing">Copper sheathing</a></div>
<p>Copper is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biostatic" title="Biostatic">biostatic</a>, meaning bacteria and many other forms of life will not grow on it. For this reason it has long been used to line parts of ships to protect against <a href="/wiki/Barnacle" title="Barnacle">barnacles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mussel" title="Mussel">mussels</a>. It was originally used pure, but has since been superseded by <a href="/wiki/Muntz_metal" title="Muntz metal">Muntz metal</a> and copper-based paint. Similarly, as discussed in <a href="/wiki/Copper_alloys_in_aquaculture" title="Copper alloys in aquaculture">copper alloys in aquaculture</a>, copper alloys have become important netting materials in the <a href="/wiki/Aquaculture" title="Aquaculture">aquaculture</a> industry because they are <a href="/wiki/Antimicrobial" title="Antimicrobial">antimicrobial</a> and prevent <a href="/wiki/Biofouling" title="Biofouling">biofouling</a>, even in extreme conditions<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1995_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1995-141">[141]</a></sup> and have strong structural and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corrosion-resistant" title="Corrosion-resistant">corrosion-resistant</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> properties in marine environments.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Antimicrobial">Antimicrobial</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Antimicrobial_properties_of_copper" title="Antimicrobial properties of copper">Antimicrobial properties of copper</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antimicrobial_copper-alloy_touch_surfaces" title="Antimicrobial copper-alloy touch surfaces">Antimicrobial copper-alloy touch surfaces</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Antimicrobial_copper-alloy_touch_surfaces" title="Antimicrobial copper-alloy touch surfaces">Copper-alloy touch surfaces</a> have natural properties that destroy a wide range of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microorganisms" title="Microorganisms">microorganisms</a> (e.g., <i><a href="/wiki/Escherichia_coli" title="Escherichia coli">E. coli</a></i> O157:H7, <a href="/wiki/Methicillin" title="Methicillin">methicillin</a>-resistant <i><a href="/wiki/Staphylococcus_aureus" title="Staphylococcus aureus">Staphylococcus aureus</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Methicillin-resistant_Staphylococcus_aureus" title="Methicillin-resistant Staphylococcus aureus">MRSA</a>), <i><a href="/wiki/Staphylococcus" title="Staphylococcus">Staphylococcus</a></i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clostridium_difficile_(bacteria)" title="Clostridium difficile (bacteria)">Clostridium difficile</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Influenza_A_virus" title="Influenza A virus">influenza A virus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adenoviridae" title="Adenoviridae">adenovirus</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Severe_acute_respiratory_syndrome_coronavirus_2" title="Severe acute respiratory syndrome coronavirus 2">SARS-Cov-2</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fungus" title="Fungus">fungi</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Copper_Touch_Surfaces_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-Copper_Touch_Surfaces-143">[143]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> Indians have been using copper vessels since ancient times for storing water, even before modern science realized its antimicrobial properties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-145">[145]</a></sup> Some copper alloys were proven to kill more than 99.9% of disease-causing bacteria within just two hours when cleaned regularly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-146">[146]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency" title="United States Environmental Protection Agency">United States Environmental Protection Agency</a> (EPA) has approved the registrations of these copper alloys as "<a href="/wiki/Antimicrobial" title="Antimicrobial">antimicrobial</a> materials with public health benefits";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_146-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-146">[146]</a></sup> that approval allows manufacturers to make legal claims to the public health benefits of products made of registered alloys. In addition, the EPA has approved a long list of antimicrobial copper products made from these alloys, such as bedrails, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Handrails" title="Handrails">handrails</a>, over-bed tables, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sinks" title="Sinks">sinks</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Faucets" title="Faucets">faucets</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Door_knobs" title="Door knobs">door knobs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Toilet" title="Toilet">toilet</a> hardware, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Computer_keyboards" title="Computer keyboards">computer keyboards</a>, <a href="/wiki/Health_club" title="Health club">health club</a> equipment, and <a href="/wiki/Shopping_cart" title="Shopping cart">shopping cart</a> handles (for a comprehensive list, see: <a href="/wiki/Antimicrobial_copper-alloy_touch_surfaces#Approved_products" title="Antimicrobial copper-alloy touch surfaces">Antimicrobial copper-alloy touch surfaces#Approved products</a>). Copper doorknobs are used by hospitals to reduce the transfer of disease, and <a href="/wiki/Legionnaires%27_disease" title="Legionnaires' disease">Legionnaires' disease</a> is suppressed by copper tubing in plumbing systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> Antimicrobial copper alloy products are now being installed in healthcare facilities in the U.K., Ireland, Japan, Korea, France, Denmark, and Brazil, as well as being called for in the US,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> and in the subway transit system in Santiago, Chile, where copper-zinc alloy handrails were installed in some 30 stations between 2011 and 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup>
Textile fibers can be blended with copper to create antimicrobial protective fabrics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Reliable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Reliable sources"><span title="The material near this tag may rely on an unreliable source. (November 2013)">unreliable source?</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Speculative_investing">Speculative investing</span></h3>
<p>Copper may be used as a speculative investment due to the predicted increase in use from worldwide infrastructure growth, and the important role it has in producing <a href="/wiki/Wind_turbine" title="Wind turbine">wind turbines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solar_panel" title="Solar panel">solar panels</a>, and other renewable energy sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> Another reason predicted demand increases is the fact that <a href="/wiki/Electric_car" title="Electric car">electric cars</a> contain an average of 3.6 times as much copper as conventional cars, although the effect of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electric_cars" title="Electric cars">electric cars</a> on copper demand is debated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> Some people invest in copper through copper mining stocks, <a href="/wiki/Exchange-traded_fund" title="Exchange-traded fund">ETFs</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Futures_contract" title="Futures contract">futures</a>. Others store physical copper in the form of copper bars or rounds although these tend to carry a higher premium in comparison to precious metals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup> Those who want to avoid the premiums of copper <a href="/wiki/Bullion" title="Bullion">bullion</a> alternatively store old <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Copper_wire" title="Copper wire">copper wire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Copper_tubing" title="Copper tubing">copper tubing</a> or American <a href="/wiki/Penny_(United_States_coin)" title="Penny (United States coin)">pennies made before 1982</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Folk_medicine">Folk medicine</span></h3>
<p>Copper is commonly used in jewelry, and according to some folklore, copper bracelets relieve <a href="/wiki/Arthritis" title="Arthritis">arthritis</a> symptoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> In one trial for osteoarthritis and one trial for rheumatoid arthritis, no differences is found between copper bracelet and control (non-copper) bracelet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RichmondBrown2009_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-RichmondBrown2009-161">[161]</a></sup> No evidence shows that copper can be absorbed through the skin. If it were, it might lead to <a href="/wiki/Copper_toxicity" title="Copper toxicity">copper poisoning</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Compression_clothing">Compression clothing</span></h4>
<p>Recently, some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Compression_bandage" title="Compression bandage">compression</a> clothing with inter-woven copper has been marketed with health claims similar to the folk medicine claims. Because compression clothing is a valid treatment for some ailments, the clothing may have that benefit, but the added copper may have no benefit beyond a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Placebo_effect" title="Placebo effect">placebo effect</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Degradation">Degradation</span></h2>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Chromobacterium_violaceum" title="Chromobacterium violaceum">Chromobacterium violaceum</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Pseudomonas_fluorescens" title="Pseudomonas fluorescens">Pseudomonas fluorescens</a></i> can both mobilize solid copper as a cyanide compound.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Geoffrey_Michael_Gadd_609–643_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-Geoffrey_Michael_Gadd_609–643-164">[164]</a></sup> The ericoid mycorrhizal fungi associated with <i>Calluna</i>, <i>Erica</i> and <i>Vaccinium</i> can grow in metalliferous soils containing copper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Geoffrey_Michael_Gadd_609–643_164-1"><a href="#cite_note-Geoffrey_Michael_Gadd_609–643-164">[164]</a></sup> The ectomycorrhizal fungus <i>Suillus luteus</i> protects young pine trees from copper toxicity. A sample of the fungus <i><a href="/wiki/Aspergillus_niger" title="Aspergillus niger">Aspergillus niger</a></i> was found growing from gold mining solution and was found to contain cyano complexes of such metals as gold, silver, copper, iron, and zinc. The fungus also plays a role in the solubilization of heavy metal sulfides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biological_role">Biological role</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Copper_in_health" title="Copper in health">Copper in health</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ARS_copper_rich_foods.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1776" data-file-width="2700" decoding="async" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/ARS_copper_rich_foods.jpg/220px-ARS_copper_rich_foods.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/ARS_copper_rich_foods.jpg/330px-ARS_copper_rich_foods.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/ARS_copper_rich_foods.jpg/440px-ARS_copper_rich_foods.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ARS_copper_rich_foods.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Rich sources of copper include oysters, beef and lamb liver, Brazil nuts, blackstrap molasses, cocoa, and black pepper. Good sources include lobster, nuts and sunflower seeds, green olives, avocados, and wheat bran.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biochemistry">Biochemistry</span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Copper_proteins" title="Copper proteins">Copper proteins</a> have diverse roles in biological electron transport and oxygen transportation, processes that exploit the easy interconversion of Cu(I) and Cu(II).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> Copper is essential in the aerobic <a href="/wiki/Cellular_respiration" title="Cellular respiration">respiration</a> of all <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eukaryotes" title="Eukaryotes">eukaryotes</a>. In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mitochondria" title="Mitochondria">mitochondria</a>, it is found in <a href="/wiki/Cytochrome_c_oxidase" title="Cytochrome c oxidase">cytochrome c oxidase</a>, which is the last protein in <a href="/wiki/Oxidative_phosphorylation" title="Oxidative phosphorylation">oxidative phosphorylation</a>. Cytochrome c oxidase is the protein that binds the O<sub>2</sub> between a copper and an iron; the protein transfers 8 electrons to the O<sub>2</sub> molecule to reduce it to two molecules of water. Copper is also found in many <a href="/wiki/Superoxide_dismutase" title="Superoxide dismutase">superoxide dismutases</a>, proteins that catalyze the decomposition of <a href="/wiki/Superoxide" title="Superoxide">superoxides</a> by converting it (by <a href="/wiki/Disproportionation" title="Disproportionation">disproportionation</a>) to oxygen and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_peroxide" title="Hydrogen peroxide">hydrogen peroxide</a>:
</p>
<ul><li>Cu<sup>2+</sup>-SOD + O<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup> → Cu<sup>+</sup>-SOD + O<sub>2</sub> (reduction of copper; oxidation of superoxide)</li>
<li>Cu<sup>+</sup>-SOD + O<sub>2</sub><sup>−</sup> + 2H<sup>+</sup> → Cu<sup>2+</sup>-SOD + H<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (oxidation of copper; reduction of superoxide)</li></ul>
<p>The protein <a href="/wiki/Hemocyanin" title="Hemocyanin">hemocyanin</a> is the oxygen carrier in most <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mollusk" title="Mollusk">mollusks</a> and some <a href="/wiki/Arthropod" title="Arthropod">arthropods</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Horseshoe_crab" title="Horseshoe crab">horseshoe crab</a> (<i>Limulus polyphemus</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NOAA_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-NOAA-167">[167]</a></sup> Because hemocyanin is blue, these organisms have blue blood rather than the red blood of iron-based <a href="/wiki/Hemoglobin" title="Hemoglobin">hemoglobin</a>. Structurally related to hemocyanin are the <a href="/wiki/Laccase" title="Laccase">laccases</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tyrosinase" title="Tyrosinase">tyrosinases</a>. Instead of reversibly binding oxygen, these proteins hydroxylate substrates, illustrated by their role in the formation of <a href="/wiki/Lacquer" title="Lacquer">lacquers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lippard_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lippard-168">[168]</a></sup> The biological role for copper commenced with the appearance of oxygen in earth's atmosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup> Several copper proteins, such as the "blue copper proteins", do not interact directly with substrates; hence they are not enzymes. These proteins relay electrons by the process called <a href="/wiki/Electron_transfer" title="Electron transfer">electron transfer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lippard_168-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lippard-168">[168]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:442px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thylakoid_membrane.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="516" data-file-width="905" decoding="async" height="251" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Thylakoid_membrane.png/440px-Thylakoid_membrane.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Thylakoid_membrane.png/660px-Thylakoid_membrane.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Thylakoid_membrane.png/880px-Thylakoid_membrane.png 2x" width="440"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thylakoid_membrane.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Photosynthesis functions by an elaborate electron transport chain within the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thylakoid_membrane" title="Thylakoid membrane">thylakoid membrane</a>. A central link in this chain is <a href="/wiki/Plastocyanin" title="Plastocyanin">plastocyanin</a>, a blue copper protein.</div></div></div>
<p>A unique tetranuclear copper center has been found in <a href="/wiki/Nitrous-oxide_reductase" title="Nitrous-oxide reductase">nitrous-oxide reductase</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup>
</p><p>Chemical compounds which were developed for treatment of Wilson's disease have been investigated for use in cancer therapy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nutrition">Nutrition</span></h3>
<p>Copper is an essential <a href="/wiki/Trace_element" title="Trace element">trace element</a> in plants and animals, but not all microorganisms. The human body contains copper at a level of about 1.4 to 2.1 mg per kg of body mass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-copper.org_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-copper.org-172">[172]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Absorption">Absorption</span></h4>
<p>Copper is absorbed in the gut, then transported to the liver bound to <a href="/wiki/Serum_albumin" title="Serum albumin">albumin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup> After processing in the liver, copper is distributed to other tissues in a second phase, which involves the protein <a href="/wiki/Ceruloplasmin" title="Ceruloplasmin">ceruloplasmin</a>, carrying the majority of copper in blood. Ceruloplasmin also carries the copper that is excreted in milk, and is particularly well-absorbed as a copper source.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup> Copper in the body normally undergoes <a href="/wiki/Enterohepatic_circulation" title="Enterohepatic circulation">enterohepatic circulation</a> (about 5 mg a day, vs. about 1 mg per day absorbed in the diet and excreted from the body), and the body is able to excrete some excess copper, if needed, via <a href="/wiki/Bile" title="Bile">bile</a>, which carries some copper out of the liver that is not then reabsorbed by the intestine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Dietary_recommendations">Dietary recommendations</span></h4>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Institute_of_Medicine" title="U.S. Institute of Medicine">U.S. Institute of Medicine</a> (IOM) updated the estimated average requirements (EARs) and recommended dietary allowances (RDAs) for copper in 2001. If there is not sufficient information to establish EARs and RDAs, an estimate designated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adequate_Intake" title="Adequate Intake">Adequate Intake</a> (AI) is used instead. The AIs for copper are: 200 μg of copper for 0–6-month-old males and females, and 220 μg of copper for 7–12-month-old males and females. For both sexes, the RDAs for copper are: 340 μg of copper for 1–3 years old, 440 μg of copper for 4–8 years old, 700 μg of copper for 9–13 years old, 890 μg of copper for 14–18 years old and 900 μg of copper for ages 19 years and older. For pregnancy, 1,000 μg. For lactation, 1,300 μg.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup> As for safety, the IOM also sets <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tolerable_upper_intake_level" title="Tolerable upper intake level">tolerable upper intake levels</a> (ULs) for vitamins and minerals when evidence is sufficient. In the case of copper the UL is set at 10 mg/day. Collectively the EARs, RDAs, AIs and ULs are referred to as <a href="/wiki/Dietary_Reference_Intake" title="Dietary Reference Intake">Dietary Reference Intakes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/European_Food_Safety_Authority" title="European Food Safety Authority">European Food Safety Authority</a> (EFSA) refers to the collective set of information as Dietary Reference Values, with Population Reference Intake (PRI) instead of RDA, and Average Requirement instead of EAR. AI and UL defined the same as in United States. For women and men ages 18 and older the AIs are set at 1.3 and 1.6 mg/day, respectively. AIs for pregnancy and lactation is 1.5 mg/day. For children ages 1–17 years the AIs increase with age from 0.7 to 1.3 mg/day. These AIs are higher than the U.S. RDAs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup> The European Food Safety Authority reviewed the same safety question and set its UL at 5 mg/day, which is half the U.S. value.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup>
</p><p>For U.S. food and dietary supplement labeling purposes the amount in a serving is expressed as a percent of Daily Value (%DV). For copper labeling purposes 100% of the Daily Value was 2.0 mg, but as of May 27, 2016<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Copper&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> it was revised to 0.9 mg to bring it into agreement with the RDA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FedReg_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-FedReg-181">[181]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup> A table of the old and new adult daily values is provided at <a href="/wiki/Reference_Daily_Intake" title="Reference Daily Intake">Reference Daily Intake</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Deficiency">Deficiency</span></h3>
<p>Because of its role in facilitating iron uptake, <a href="/wiki/Copper_deficiency" title="Copper deficiency">copper deficiency</a> can produce <a href="/wiki/Anemia" title="Anemia">anemia</a>-like symptoms, <a href="/wiki/Neutropenia" title="Neutropenia">neutropenia</a>, bone abnormalities, hypopigmentation, impaired growth, increased incidence of infections, osteoporosis, hyperthyroidism, and abnormalities in glucose and cholesterol metabolism. Conversely, <a href="/wiki/Wilson%27s_disease" title="Wilson's disease">Wilson's disease</a> causes an accumulation of copper in body tissues.
</p><p>Severe deficiency can be found by testing for low plasma or serum copper levels, low ceruloplasmin, and low red blood cell superoxide dismutase levels; these are not sensitive to marginal copper status. The "cytochrome c oxidase activity of leucocytes and platelets" has been stated as another factor in deficiency, but the results have not been confirmed by replication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bonhametal2002_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bonhametal2002-183">[183]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Toxicity">Toxicity</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Copper_toxicity" title="Copper toxicity">Copper toxicity</a></div>
<p>Gram quantities of various copper salts have been taken in suicide attempts and produced acute copper toxicity in humans, possibly due to redox cycling and the generation of <a href="/wiki/Reactive_oxygen_species" title="Reactive oxygen species">reactive oxygen species</a> that damage <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup> Corresponding amounts of copper salts (30 mg/kg) are toxic in animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup> A minimum dietary value for healthy growth in rabbits has been reported to be at least 3 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parts_per_million" title="Parts per million">ppm</a> in the diet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup> However, higher concentrations of copper (100 ppm, 200 ppm, or 500 ppm) in the diet of rabbits may favorably influence <a href="/wiki/Feed_conversion_ratio" title="Feed conversion ratio">feed conversion efficiency</a>, growth rates, and carcass dressing percentages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup>
</p><p>Chronic copper toxicity does not normally occur in humans because of transport systems that regulate absorption and excretion. Autosomal recessive mutations in copper transport proteins can disable these systems, leading to <a href="/wiki/Wilson%27s_disease" title="Wilson's disease">Wilson's disease</a> with copper accumulation and <a href="/wiki/Cirrhosis" title="Cirrhosis">cirrhosis</a> of the liver in persons who have inherited two defective genes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-copper.org_172-1"><a href="#cite_note-copper.org-172">[172]</a></sup>
</p><p>Elevated copper levels have also been linked to worsening symptoms of <a href="/wiki/Alzheimer%27s_disease" title="Alzheimer's disease">Alzheimer's disease</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_exposure">Human exposure</span></h3>
<p>In the US, the <a href="/wiki/Occupational_Safety_and_Health_Administration" title="Occupational Safety and Health Administration">Occupational Safety and Health Administration</a> (OSHA) has designated a <a href="/wiki/Permissible_exposure_limit" title="Permissible exposure limit">permissible exposure limit</a> (PEL) for copper dust and fumes in the workplace as a time-weighted average (TWA) of 1 mg/m<sup>3</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_for_Occupational_Safety_and_Health" title="National Institute for Occupational Safety and Health">National Institute for Occupational Safety and Health</a> (NIOSH) has set a <a href="/wiki/Recommended_exposure_limit" title="Recommended exposure limit">recommended exposure limit</a> (REL) of 1 mg/m<sup>3</sup>, time-weighted average. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IDLH" title="IDLH">IDLH</a> (immediately dangerous to life and health) value is 100 mg/m<sup>3</sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup>
</p><p>Copper is a constituent of <a href="/wiki/Tobacco_smoke" title="Tobacco smoke">tobacco smoke</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TalhoutSchulz2011_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-TalhoutSchulz2011-194">[194]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tobacco_plant" title="Tobacco plant">tobacco plant</a> readily absorbs and accumulates <a href="/wiki/Heavy_metals" title="Heavy metals">heavy metals</a>, such as copper from the surrounding soil into its leaves. These are readily absorbed into the user's body following smoke inhalation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup> The health implications are not clear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-162"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-162">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">University of Arkansas for Medical Sciences:<br/><a class="external text" href="http://www.uams.edu/update/absolutenm/templates/medical.asp?articleid=3454" rel="nofollow">Find the Truth Behind Medical Myths</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20140106233901/http://www.uams.edu/update/absolutenm/templates/medical.asp?articleid=3454" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 6 January 2014 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a><br/><br/>While it's never been proven that copper can be absorbed through the skin by wearing a bracelet, research has shown that excessive copper can result in poisoning, causing vomiting and, in severe cases, liver damage.</span>
